The question is asking how much work must be done on a particle of mass m to accelerate it to about 0.6c, assuming it starts from rest. The options are A. 0.25mc², B. 0.60mc², C. 0.67mc², and D. 1.25mc². The correct answer is supposed to be D, 1.25mc², but I need to figure out why.

Hmm, I remember that in relativity, when dealing with speeds close to the speed of light, you can't just use the classical formula for kinetic energy because it doesn't account for the relativistic effects. So, I think I need to use the relativistic work-energy theorem here.

First, let me recall that the total work done on a particle is equal to its total energy minus its rest energy. That is, W = E_total - E_rest. So, I need to compute the total energy of the proton at 0.6c and subtract its rest energy.

The rest energy of the proton is just mc², where m is its rest mass and c is the speed of light. So, E_rest = mc².

For the total energy, E_total, I believe it's given by the gamma factor multiplied by the rest energy. The gamma factor is 1 / sqrt(1 - v²/c²). Let me write that down:

gamma = 1 / sqrt(1 - v²/c²)

So, plugging in v = 0.6c, let's calculate gamma. Let's compute the denominator first:

1 - (0.6c)² / c² = 1 - 0.36 = 0.64

So, sqrt(0.64) is 0.8. Therefore, gamma = 1 / 0.8 = 1.25

So, the total energy E_total = gamma * mc² = 1.25mc²

Therefore, the work done W = E_total - E_rest = 1.25mc² - mc² = 0.25mc²

Wait, I think I need to check the work-energy theorem in relativity.

The work-energy theorem states that the work done on a particle is equal to its change in kinetic energy.

In relativity, the kinetic energy is (gamma - 1) mc².

So, if the particle starts at rest, its initial kinetic energy is 0, and the final kinetic energy is (gamma - 1) mc².

Therefore, the work done is (gamma - 1) mc².

So, with gamma = 1.25, the work done is 0.25mc².

Therefore, the correct answer is A.

But, from the definition of gamma, gamma = 1 / sqrt(1 - v²/c²).

So, gamma' = d gamma / dv = (1/2) (1 - v²/c²)^(-3/2) * (2v/c²) = v/(c² sqrt(1 - v²/c²)).

So, dp/dt = m v (v / (c² sqrt(1 - v²/c²))) dv/dt + gamma m dv/dt.

dp/dt = [m v² / (c² sqrt(1 - v²/c²))] dv/dt + [gamma m] dv/dt.

So, F = dp/dt = [m / sqrt(1 - v²/c²)] dv/dt [1 + v²/c²].

Since the speed is a significant fraction of the speed of light (0.6c), we definitely need to use the relativistic energy formulas here.

So, the basic idea is that the work done on the particle is equal to its kinetic energy. In relativity, kinetic energy (KE) isn't just (1/2)mv² like in classical mechanics. Instead, it's given by the total energy minus the rest mass energy. The total energy (E) is related to the rest mass (m) and the Lorentz factor (γ), which is 1 over the square root of (1 - v²/c²). Therefore, KE = (γ - 1)mc².

Alright, so let's write that down:

KE = (γ - 1)mc².

First, we need to find the Lorentz factor γ for v = 0.6c.

Calculating γ:

γ = 1 / sqrt(1 - v²/c²)  
= 1 / sqrt(1 - (0.6c)²/c²)  
= 1 / sqrt(1 - 0.36)  
= 1 / sqrt(0.64)  
= 1 / 0.8  
= 1.25.

So, γ is 1.25.

Now, plugging that back into the KE formula:

KE = (1.25 - 1)mc²  
= 0.25mc².

It says they're accelerated to about 0.6c, and the question is asking how much work must be done on a particle of mass m to reach that speed, assuming it starts from rest. The options are A. 0.25mc², B. 0.60mc², C. 0.67mc², D. 1.25mc². And apparently, the correct answer is D, 1.25mc². Hmm, I need to figure out why.

Alright, let's start by recalling some basic concepts. This seems like a problem involving relativistic speeds because the speed is a significant fraction of the speed of light (c ≈ 3 x 10⁸ m/s). When dealing with speeds close to c, we can't use the classical equations of motion; we have to use the ones from special relativity.

First, I remember that work done on a particle is related to the change in its kinetic energy. In classical mechanics, work is equal to the change in kinetic energy, but in relativity, it's a bit different because the relationship between work and energy isn't as straightforward.

So, the total energy of a particle in relativity is given by the equation:

E = γmc²

where γ (gamma) is the Lorentz factor, which is:

γ = 1 / √(1 - v²/c²)

Since the particle starts from rest, its initial energy is just its rest energy, which is mc². After being accelerated to speed v, its total energy becomes E = γmc². Therefore, the work done on the particle is the difference between its final energy and its initial energy.

So, work done (W) = E_final - E_initial = γmc² - mc² = (γ - 1)mc²

Alright, so I need to calculate (γ - 1)mc². The problem states that the particle is accelerated to 0.6c, so v = 0.6c.

Let me compute γ first. Plugging v = 0.6c into the Lorentz factor:

γ = 1 / √(1 - (0.6c)² / c²) = 1 / √(1 - 0.36) = 1 / √0.64

√0.64 is 0.8, so γ = 1 / 0.8 = 1.25

Therefore, (γ - 1) = 1.25 - 1 = 0.25

So, W = 0.25mc²

The Lorentz factor is defined as γ = 1 / sqrt(1 - v²/c²).

Work = (γ - 1)mc²

Work = (1.25 - 1)mc² = 0.25mc²
